1 The woman patted and pinned and fixed and joined , and then , turning to the man , she said : ` Now it is ready for you to try on . '
2 And she made him take off his coat , and stand up in front of her , and once more she patted an pinned and fixed and joined , and was very careful in smoothing out every wrinkle .
3 ` It does not feel very warm , ' observed the man at last , when he had borne all this patiently for a long time .
4 ` That is because it is so fine , ' answered she ; ` you do not want it to be as thick as the rough clothes you wear every day . '
5 He DID , but was ashamed to say so , and only answered : ` Well , I am sure it must be beautiful since you say so , and I shall be smarter than anyone in the whole village .
6 `` What a splendid coat ! ''
7 they will exclaim when they see me .
8 But it is not everybody who has a wife as clever as mine . '
9 Meanwhile the other wife was not idle .
10 As soon as her husband entered she looked at him with such a look of terror that the poor man was quite frightened .
11 ` Why do you stare at me so ?
12 Is there anything the matter ? '
13 asked he .
14 ` Oh !
15 go to bed at once , ' she cried ; ` you must be very ill indeed to look like that ! '
16 The man was rather surprised at first , as he felt particularly well that evening ; but the moment his wife spoke he became quite certain that he had something dreadful the matter with him , and grew quite pale . '
17 I dare say it would be the best place for me , ' he answered , trembling ; and he suffered his wife to take him upstairs , and to help him off with his clothes .
18 ` If you sleep well during the might there MAY be a chance for you , ' said she , shaking her head , as she tucked him up warmly ; ` but if not -- ' And of course the poor man never closed an eye till the sun rose .
19 ` How do you feel this morning ? '
20 asked the woman , coming in on tip-toe when her house-work was finished .
21 ` Oh , bad ; very bad indeed , ' answered he ; ' I have not slept for a XXXXX .	moment		anyone|chance|coat|course|eye|matter|mine|moment|sun|village

1 ` How do you feel this morning ? '
2 asked the woman , coming in on tip-toe when her house-work was finished .
3 ` Oh , bad ; very bad indeed , ' answered he ; ' I have not slept for a moment .
4 Can you think of nothing to make me better ? ' '
5 I will try everything that is possible , ' said the wife , who did not in the least wish her husband to die , but was determined to show that he was more foolish that the other man . '
6 I will get some dried herbs and make you a drink , but I am very much afraid that it is too late .
7 Why did you not tell me before ? ' '
8 I thought perhaps the pain would go off in a day or two ; and , besides , I did not want to make you unhappy , ' answered the man , who was by this time quite sure he had been suffering tortures , and had borne them like a hero .
9 ` Of course , if I had had any idea how ill I really was , I should have spoken at once . '
10 ` Well , well , I will see what can be done , ' said the wife , ` but talking is not good for you .
11 Lie still , and keep yourself warm . '
12 All that day the man lay in bed , and whenever his wife entered the room and asked him , with a shake of the head , how he felt , he always replied that he was getting worse .
13 At last , in the evening , she burst into tears , and when he inquired what was the matter , she sobbed out : ` Oh , my poor , poor husband , are you really dead ?
14 I must go to-morrow and order your coffin . '
15 Now , when the man heard this , a cold shiver ran through his body , and all at once he knew that he was as well as he had ever been in his life .
16 ` Oh , no , no ! '
17 he cried , ' I feel quite recovered !
18 Indeed , I think I shall go out to work . '
19 ` You will do no such thing , ' replied his wife .
20 ` Just keep quite quiet , for before the sun rises you will be a dead man . '

7 Why did you not tell me before ? ' '

9 ` Of course , if I had had any idea how ill I really was , I should have spoken at once . '
10 ` Well , well , I will see what can be done , ' said the wife , ` but talking is not good for you .
11 Lie still , and keep yourself warm . '
12 All that day the man lay in bed , and whenever his wife entered the room and asked him , with a shake of the head , how he felt , he always replied that he was getting worse .
13 At last , in the evening , she burst into tears , and when he inquired what was the matter , she sobbed out : ` Oh , my poor , poor husband , are you really dead ?
14 I must go to-morrow and order your coffin . '
15 Now , when the man heard this , a cold shiver ran through his body , and all at once he knew that he was as well as he had ever been in his life .
16 ` Oh , no , no ! '
17 he cried , ' I feel quite recovered !
18 Indeed , I think I shall go out to work . '
19 ` You will do no such thing , ' replied his wife .
20 ` Just keep quite quiet , for before the sun rises you will be a dead man . '
21 The man was very frightened at her words , and lay absolutely still while the undertaker came and measured him for his XXXXX ; and his wife gave orders to the gravedigger about his grave .	coffin		body|coffin|herbs|hero|life|man|matter|nothing|thing|wish

18 ` Were you really not dead after all ? '

20 ` And if not , why did you let yourself be buried ? '

1 All that day the man lay in bed , and whenever his wife entered the room and asked him , with a shake of the head , how he felt , he always replied that he was getting worse .
2 At last , in the evening , she burst into tears , and when he inquired what was the matter , she sobbed out : ` Oh , my poor , poor husband , are you really dead ?
3 I must go to-morrow and order your coffin . '
4 Now , when the man heard this , a cold shiver ran through his body , and all at once he knew that he was as well as he had ever been in his life .
5 ` Oh , no , no ! '
6 he cried , ' I feel quite recovered !
7 Indeed , I think I shall go out to work . '
8 ` You will do no such thing , ' replied his wife .
9 ` Just keep quite quiet , for before the sun rises you will be a dead man . '
10 The man was very frightened at her words , and lay absolutely still while the undertaker came and measured him for his coffin ; and his wife gave orders to the gravedigger about his grave .
11 That evening the coffin was sent home , and in the morning at nine o'clock the woman put him on a long flannel garment , and called to the undertaker 's men to fasten down the lid and carry him to the grave , where all their friends were waiting them .
12 Just as the body was being placed in the ground the other woman 's husband came running up , dressed , as far as anyone could see , in no clothes at all .
13 Everybody burst into shouts of laughter at the sight of him , and the men laid down the coffin and laughed too , till their sides nearly split .
14 The dead man was so astonished at this behaviour , that he peeped out of a little window in the side of the coffin , and cried out : ' I should laugh as loudly as any of you , if I were not a dead man . '
15 When they heard the voice coming from the coffin the other people suddenly stopped laughing , and stood as if they had been turned into stone .
16 Then they rushed with one accord to the coffin , and lifted the lid so that the man could step out amongst them .
17 ` Were you really not dead after all ? '
18 asked they .
19 ` And if not , why did you let yourself be buried ? '
20 At this the wives both confessed that they had each wished to prove that her husband was stupider than the other .
21 But the villagers declared that they could not decide which was the most foolish -- the XXXXX who allowed himself to be persuaded that he was wearing fine clothes when he was dressed in nothing , or the man who let himself be buried when he was alive and well .	man		coffin|ground|laugh|life|man|people|side|thing|undertaker|window

16 ` Were you really not dead after all ? '
17 asked they .
18 ` And if not , why did you let yourself be buried ? '
19 At this the wives both confessed that they had each wished to prove that her husband was stupider than the other .
20 But the villagers declared that they could not decide which was the most foolish -- the man who allowed himself to be persuaded that he was wearing fine clothes when he was dressed in nothing , or the man who let himself be buried when he was alive and well .
21 So the women quarrelled just as much as they did before , and no one ever knew whose XXXXX was the most foolish .	husband		anyone|behaviour|clothes|husband|life|matter|wife|window|wives|woman

1 ` Oh , no , no ! '
2 he cried , ' I feel quite recovered !
3 Indeed , I think I shall go out to work . '
4 ` You will do no such thing , ' replied his wife .
5 ` Just keep quite quiet , for before the sun rises you will be a dead man . '
6 The man was very frightened at her words , and lay absolutely still while the undertaker came and measured him for his coffin ; and his wife gave orders to the gravedigger about his grave .
7 That evening the coffin was sent home , and in the morning at nine o'clock the woman put him on a long flannel garment , and called to the undertaker 's men to fasten down the lid and carry him to the grave , where all their friends were waiting them .
8 Just as the body was being placed in the ground the other woman 's husband came running up , dressed , as far as anyone could see , in no clothes at all .
9 Everybody burst into shouts of laughter at the sight of him , and the men laid down the coffin and laughed too , till their sides nearly split .

16 At this the wives both confessed that they had each wished to prove that her husband was stupider than the other .
17 But the villagers declared that they could not decide which was the most foolish -- the man who allowed himself to be persuaded that he was wearing fine clothes when he was dressed in nothing , or the man who let himself be buried when he was alive and well .
18 So the women quarrelled just as much as they did before , and no one ever knew whose husband was the most foolish .

10 Asmund and Signy Long , long ago , in the days when fairies , witches , giants and ogres still visited the earth , there lived a king who reigned over a great and beautiful country .
11 He was married to a wife whom he dearly loved , and had two most promising children -- a son called Asmund , and a daughter who was named Signy .
12 The king and queen were very anxious to bring their children up well , and the young prince and princess were taught everything likely to make them clever and accomplished .
13 They lived at home in their father 's palace , and he spared no pains to make their lives happy .
14 Prince Asmund dearly loved all outdoor sports and an open-air life , and from his earliest childhood he had longed to live entirely in the forest close by .
15 After many arguments and entreaties he succeeded in persuading the king to give him two great oak trees for his very own .
16 ` Now , ' said he to his sister , ' I will have the trees hollowed out , and then I will make rooms in them and furnish them so that I shall be able to live out in the forest . '
17 ` Oh , Asmund ! '
18 exclaimed Signy , ` what a delightful idea !
19 Do let me come too , and live in one of your trees .
20 I will bring all my pretty things and ornaments , and the trees are so near home we shall be quite safe in them . '
21 Asmund , who was extremely fond of his XXXXX , readily consented , and they had a very happy time together , carrying over all their pet treasures , and Signy 's jewels and other ornaments , and arranging them in the pretty little rooms inside the trees .	sister		coffin|father|home|life|queen|sister|sports|trees|voice|wives

1 ` Were you really not dead after all ? '
2 asked they .
3 ` And if not , why did you let yourself be buried ? '
4 At this the wives both confessed that they had each wished to prove that her husband was stupider than the other .
5 But the villagers declared that they could not decide which was the most foolish -- the man who allowed himself to be persuaded that he was wearing fine clothes when he was dressed in nothing , or the man who let himself be buried when he was alive and well .
6 So the women quarrelled just as much as they did before , and no one ever knew whose husband was the most foolish .

21 So he begged his father to let him have a ship for the voyage , set sail with a favourable wind , and after a time landed in the XXXXX where Signy lived .	country		arguments|children|country|daughter|giants|home|idea|minds|ornaments|palace

1 -LSB- Adapted from the Neuislandische Volksmarchen . -RSB-
2 Asmund and Signy Long , long ago , in the days when fairies , witches , giants and ogres still visited the earth , there lived a king who reigned over a great and beautiful country .
3 He was married to a wife whom he dearly loved , and had two most promising children -- a son called Asmund , and a daughter who was named Signy .
4 The king and queen were very anxious to bring their children up well , and the young prince and princess were taught everything likely to make them clever and accomplished .
5 They lived at home in their father 's palace , and he spared no pains to make their lives happy .
6 Prince Asmund dearly loved all outdoor sports and an open-air life , and from his earliest childhood he had longed to live entirely in the forest close by .
7 After many arguments and entreaties he succeeded in persuading the king to give him two great oak trees for his very own .
8 ` Now , ' said he to his sister , ' I will have the trees hollowed out , and then I will make rooms in them and furnish them so that I shall be able to live out in the forest . '
9 ` Oh , Asmund ! '
10 exclaimed Signy , ` what a delightful idea !
11 Do let me come too , and live in one of your trees .
12 I will bring all my pretty things and ornaments , and the trees are so near home we shall be quite safe in them . '
13 Asmund , who was extremely fond of his sister , readily consented , and they had a very happy time together , carrying over all their pet treasures , and Signy 's jewels and other ornaments , and arranging them in the pretty little rooms inside the trees .
14 Unfortunately sadder days were to come .
15 A war with another country broke out , and the king had to lead his army against their enemy .
16 During his absence the queen fell ill , and after lingering for some time she died , to the great grief of her children .
17 They made up their minds to live altogether for a time in their trees , and for this purpose they had provisions enough stored up inside to last them a year .
18 Now , I must tell you , in another country a long way off , there reigned a king who had an only son named Ring .
19 Prince Ring had heard so much about the beauty and goodness of Princess Signy that he determined to marry her if possible .
20 So he begged his father to let him have a ship for the voyage , set sail with a favourable wind , and after a time landed in the country where Signy lived .
21 The XXXXX lost no time in setting out for the royal palace , and on his way there he met such a wonderfully lovely woman that he felt he had never seen such beauty in all his life .	prince		beauty|country|life|ornaments|prince|queen|son|sports|things|trees

4 ` Oh , Asmund ! '
5 exclaimed Signy , ` what a delightful idea !
6 Do let me come too , and live in one of your trees .
7 I will bring all my pretty things and ornaments , and the trees are so near home we shall be quite safe in them . '
8 Asmund , who was extremely fond of his sister , readily consented , and they had a very happy time together , carrying over all their pet treasures , and Signy 's jewels and other ornaments , and arranging them in the pretty little rooms inside the trees .
9 Unfortunately sadder days were to come .
10 A war with another country broke out , and the king had to lead his army against their enemy .
11 During his absence the queen fell ill , and after lingering for some time she died , to the great grief of her children .
12 They made up their minds to live altogether for a time in their trees , and for this purpose they had provisions enough stored up inside to last them a year .
13 Now , I must tell you , in another country a long way off , there reigned a king who had an only son named Ring .
14 Prince Ring had heard so much about the beauty and goodness of Princess Signy that he determined to marry her if possible .
15 So he begged his father to let him have a ship for the voyage , set sail with a favourable wind , and after a time landed in the country where Signy lived .
16 The prince lost no time in setting out for the royal palace , and on his way there he met such a wonderfully lovely woman that he felt he had never seen such beauty in all his life .
17 He stopped her and at once asked who she was . '
18 I am Signy , the king 's daughter , ' was the reply .
19 Then the prince inquired why she was wandering about all by herself , and she told him that since her mother 's death she was so sad that whilst her father was away she preferred being alone .
20 Ring was quite deceived by her , and never guessed that she was not Princess Signy at all , but a strong , gigantic , wicked witch bent on deceiving him under a beautiful shape .
21 He confided to her that he had travelled all the way from his own country for her sake , having fallen in love with the accounts he had heard of her XXXXX , and he then and there asked her to be his wife .	beauty		Ring|beauty|children|father|home|king|things|time|trees|wind

2 A war with another country broke out , and the king had to lead his army against their enemy .
3 During his absence the queen fell ill , and after lingering for some time she died , to the great grief of her children .
4 They made up their minds to live altogether for a time in their trees , and for this purpose they had provisions enough stored up inside to last them a year .
5 Now , I must tell you , in another country a long way off , there reigned a king who had an only son named Ring .
6 Prince Ring had heard so much about the beauty and goodness of Princess Signy that he determined to marry her if possible .
7 So he begged his father to let him have a ship for the voyage , set sail with a favourable wind , and after a time landed in the country where Signy lived .
8 The prince lost no time in setting out for the royal palace , and on his way there he met such a wonderfully lovely woman that he felt he had never seen such beauty in all his life .
9 He stopped her and at once asked who she was . '
10 I am Signy , the king 's daughter , ' was the reply .
11 Then the prince inquired why she was wandering about all by herself , and she told him that since her mother 's death she was so sad that whilst her father was away she preferred being alone .
12 Ring was quite deceived by her , and never guessed that she was not Princess Signy at all , but a strong , gigantic , wicked witch bent on deceiving him under a beautiful shape .
13 He confided to her that he had travelled all the way from his own country for her sake , having fallen in love with the accounts he had heard of her beauty , and he then and there asked her to be his wife .
14 The witch listened to all he said and , much pleased , ended by accepting his offer ; but she begged him to return to his ship for a little while as she wished to go some way further into the forest , promising to join him later on .
15 Prince Ring did as she wished and went back to his ship to wait , whilst she walked on into the forest till she reached the two oak trees .
16 Here she resumed her own gigantic shape , tore up the trees by their roots , threw one of them over her back and clasped the other to her breast , carried them down to the shore and waded out with them to the ship .
17 She took care not to be noticed as she reached the ship , and directly she got on board she once more changed to her former lovely appearance and told the prince that her luggage was now all on board , and that they need wait for nothing more .
18 The prince gave orders to set sail at once , and after a fine voyage landed in his own country , where his parents and his only sister received him with the greatest joy and affection .
19 The false Signy was also very kindly welcomed .
20 A beautiful house was got ready for her , and Prince Ring had the two oaks planted in the garden just in front of her windows so that she might have the pleasure of seeing them constantly .
21 He often went to visit the XXXXX , whom he believed to be Princess Signy , and one day he asked : ` Do n't you think we might be married before long ? '	witch		army|death|garden|goodness|mother|oaks|pleasure|purpose|queen|witch

8 I am Signy , the king 's daughter , ' was the reply .
9 Then the prince inquired why she was wandering about all by herself , and she told him that since her mother 's death she was so sad that whilst her father was away she preferred being alone .
10 Ring was quite deceived by her , and never guessed that she was not Princess Signy at all , but a strong , gigantic , wicked witch bent on deceiving him under a beautiful shape .
11 He confided to her that he had travelled all the way from his own country for her sake , having fallen in love with the accounts he had heard of her beauty , and he then and there asked her to be his wife .
12 The witch listened to all he said and , much pleased , ended by accepting his offer ; but she begged him to return to his ship for a little while as she wished to go some way further into the forest , promising to join him later on .
13 Prince Ring did as she wished and went back to his ship to wait , whilst she walked on into the forest till she reached the two oak trees .
14 Here she resumed her own gigantic shape , tore up the trees by their roots , threw one of them over her back and clasped the other to her breast , carried them down to the shore and waded out with them to the ship .
15 She took care not to be noticed as she reached the ship , and directly she got on board she once more changed to her former lovely appearance and told the prince that her luggage was now all on board , and that they need wait for nothing more .
16 The prince gave orders to set sail at once , and after a fine voyage landed in his own country , where his parents and his only sister received him with the greatest joy and affection .
17 The false Signy was also very kindly welcomed .
18 A beautiful house was got ready for her , and Prince Ring had the two oaks planted in the garden just in front of her windows so that she might have the pleasure of seeing them constantly .
19 He often went to visit the witch , whom he believed to be Princess Signy , and one day he asked : ` Do n't you think we might be married before long ? '
20 ` Yes , ' said she , quite pleased , ' I am quite ready to marry you whenever you like . '
21 ` Then , ' replied XXXXX , ` let us decide on this day fortnight .	Ring		Ring|breast|daughter|goodness|house|king|offer|ship|wind|windows

1 Prince Ring had heard so much about the beauty and goodness of Princess Signy that he determined to marry her if possible .
2 So he begged his father to let him have a ship for the voyage , set sail with a favourable wind , and after a time landed in the country where Signy lived .
3 The prince lost no time in setting out for the royal palace , and on his way there he met such a wonderfully lovely woman that he felt he had never seen such beauty in all his life .
4 He stopped her and at once asked who she was . '
5 I am Signy , the king 's daughter , ' was the reply .
6 Then the prince inquired why she was wandering about all by herself , and she told him that since her mother 's death she was so sad that whilst her father was away she preferred being alone .
7 Ring was quite deceived by her , and never guessed that she was not Princess Signy at all , but a strong , gigantic , wicked witch bent on deceiving him under a beautiful shape .
8 He confided to her that he had travelled all the way from his own country for her sake , having fallen in love with the accounts he had heard of her beauty , and he then and there asked her to be his wife .
9 The witch listened to all he said and , much pleased , ended by accepting his offer ; but she begged him to return to his ship for a little while as she wished to go some way further into the forest , promising to join him later on .
10 Prince Ring did as she wished and went back to his ship to wait , whilst she walked on into the forest till she reached the two oak trees .
11 Here she resumed her own gigantic shape , tore up the trees by their roots , threw one of them over her back and clasped the other to her breast , carried them down to the shore and waded out with them to the ship .
12 She took care not to be noticed as she reached the ship , and directly she got on board she once more changed to her former lovely appearance and told the prince that her luggage was now all on board , and that they need wait for nothing more .
13 The prince gave orders to set sail at once , and after a fine voyage landed in his own country , where his parents and his only sister received him with the greatest joy and affection .
14 The false Signy was also very kindly welcomed .
15 A beautiful house was got ready for her , and Prince Ring had the two oaks planted in the garden just in front of her windows so that she might have the pleasure of seeing them constantly .
16 He often went to visit the witch , whom he believed to be Princess Signy , and one day he asked : ` Do n't you think we might be married before long ? '
17 ` Yes , ' said she , quite pleased , ' I am quite ready to marry you whenever you like . '
18 ` Then , ' replied Ring , ` let us decide on this day fortnight .
19 And see , I have brought you some stuff to make your wedding-dress of . '
20 So saying he gave her a large piece of the most beautiful brocade , all woven over with gold threads , and embroidered with pearls and other jewels .

6 The false Signy was also very kindly welcomed .
7 A beautiful house was got ready for her , and Prince Ring had the two oaks planted in the garden just in front of her windows so that she might have the pleasure of seeing them constantly .
8 He often went to visit the witch , whom he believed to be Princess Signy , and one day he asked : ` Do n't you think we might be married before long ? '
9 ` Yes , ' said she , quite pleased , ' I am quite ready to marry you whenever you like . '
10 ` Then , ' replied Ring , ` let us decide on this day fortnight .
11 And see , I have brought you some stuff to make your wedding-dress of . '
12 So saying he gave her a large piece of the most beautiful brocade , all woven over with gold threads , and embroidered with pearls and other jewels .
13 The prince had hardly left her before the witch resumed her proper shape and tore about the room , raging and storming and flinging the beautiful silk on the floor .
14 ` What was SHE to do with such things ? '
15 she roared .
16 ` SHE did not know how to sew or make clothes , and she was sure to die of starvation into the bargain if her brother Ironhead did not come soon and bring her some raw meat and bones , for she really could eat nothing else . '
17 As she was raving and roaring in this frantic manner part of the floor suddenly opened and a huge giant rose up carrying a great chest in his arms .
18 The witch was enchanted at this sight , and eagerly helped her brother to set down and open the chest , which was full of the ghastly food she had been longing for .
19 The horrid pair set to and greedily devoured it all , and when the chest was quite empty the giant put it on his shoulder and disappeared as he had come , without leaving any trace of his visit .
20 But his sister did not keep quiet for long , and tore and pulled at the rich brocade as if she wanted to destroy it , stamping about and shouting angrily .
21 Now , all this time Prince Asmund and his sister sat in their XXXXX just outside the window and saw all that was going on .	trees		board|brocade|chest|clothes|forest|ship|sight|trace|trees|windows

2 The prince gave orders to set sail at once , and after a fine voyage landed in his own country , where his parents and his only sister received him with the greatest joy and affection .
3 The false Signy was also very kindly welcomed .
4 A beautiful house was got ready for her , and Prince Ring had the two oaks planted in the garden just in front of her windows so that she might have the pleasure of seeing them constantly .
5 He often went to visit the witch , whom he believed to be Princess Signy , and one day he asked : ` Do n't you think we might be married before long ? '
6 ` Yes , ' said she , quite pleased , ' I am quite ready to marry you whenever you like . '
7 ` Then , ' replied Ring , ` let us decide on this day fortnight .
8 And see , I have brought you some stuff to make your wedding-dress of . '
9 So saying he gave her a large piece of the most beautiful brocade , all woven over with gold threads , and embroidered with pearls and other jewels .
10 The prince had hardly left her before the witch resumed her proper shape and tore about the room , raging and storming and flinging the beautiful silk on the floor .
11 ` What was SHE to do with such things ? '
12 she roared .
13 ` SHE did not know how to sew or make clothes , and she was sure to die of starvation into the bargain if her brother Ironhead did not come soon and bring her some raw meat and bones , for she really could eat nothing else . '
14 As she was raving and roaring in this frantic manner part of the floor suddenly opened and a huge giant rose up carrying a great chest in his arms .
15 The witch was enchanted at this sight , and eagerly helped her brother to set down and open the chest , which was full of the ghastly food she had been longing for .
16 The horrid pair set to and greedily devoured it all , and when the chest was quite empty the giant put it on his shoulder and disappeared as he had come , without leaving any trace of his visit .
17 But his sister did not keep quiet for long , and tore and pulled at the rich brocade as if she wanted to destroy it , stamping about and shouting angrily .
18 Now , all this time Prince Asmund and his sister sat in their trees just outside the window and saw all that was going on .
19 ` Dear Signy , ' said Asmund , ` do try to get hold of that piece of brocade and make the clothes yourself , for really we shall have no rest day or night with such a noise . ' '
20 I will try , ' said Signy ; ` it wo n't be an easy matter , but it 's worth while taking some trouble to have a little peace . '
21 So she watched for an opportunity and managed to carry off the XXXXX the first time the witch left her room .	brocade		brocade|brother|front|house|pair|shoulder|trees|visit|windows|witch

9 The witch was enchanted at this sight , and eagerly helped her brother to set down and open the chest , which was full of the ghastly food she had been longing for .
10 The horrid pair set to and greedily devoured it all , and when the chest was quite empty the giant put it on his shoulder and disappeared as he had come , without leaving any trace of his visit .
11 But his sister did not keep quiet for long , and tore and pulled at the rich brocade as if she wanted to destroy it , stamping about and shouting angrily .
12 Now , all this time Prince Asmund and his sister sat in their trees just outside the window and saw all that was going on .
13 ` Dear Signy , ' said Asmund , ` do try to get hold of that piece of brocade and make the clothes yourself , for really we shall have no rest day or night with such a noise . ' '
14 I will try , ' said Signy ; ` it wo n't be an easy matter , but it 's worth while taking some trouble to have a little peace . '
15 So she watched for an opportunity and managed to carry off the brocade the first time the witch left her room .
16 Then she set to work , cutting out and sewing as best she could , and by the end of six days she had turned it into an elegant robe with a long train and a mantle .
17 When it was finished she climbed to the top of her tree and contrived to throw the clothes on to a table through the open window .
18 How delighted the witch was when she found the clothes all finished !
19 The next time Prince Ring came to see her she gave them to him , and he paid her many compliments on her skilful work , after which he took leave of her in the most friendly manner .
20 But he had scarcely left the house when the witch began to rage as furiously as ever , and never stopped till her brother Ironhead appeared .
21 When Asmund saw all these wild doings from his XXXXX he felt he could no longer keep silence .	tree		Ring|brother|pair|piece|silk|top|train|tree|trees|trouble

5 ` Dear Signy , ' said Asmund , ` do try to get hold of that piece of brocade and make the clothes yourself , for really we shall have no rest day or night with such a noise . ' '
6 I will try , ' said Signy ; ` it wo n't be an easy matter , but it 's worth while taking some trouble to have a little peace . '
7 So she watched for an opportunity and managed to carry off the brocade the first time the witch left her room .
8 Then she set to work , cutting out and sewing as best she could , and by the end of six days she had turned it into an elegant robe with a long train and a mantle .
9 When it was finished she climbed to the top of her tree and contrived to throw the clothes on to a table through the open window .
10 How delighted the witch was when she found the clothes all finished !
11 The next time Prince Ring came to see her she gave them to him , and he paid her many compliments on her skilful work , after which he took leave of her in the most friendly manner .
12 But he had scarcely left the house when the witch began to rage as furiously as ever , and never stopped till her brother Ironhead appeared .
13 When Asmund saw all these wild doings from his tree he felt he could no longer keep silence .
14 He went to Prince Ring and said : ` Do come with me and see the strange things that are happening in the new princess 's room . '
15 The prince was not a little surprised , but he consented to hide himself with Asmund behind the panelling of the room , from where they could see all that went on through a little slit .
16 The witch was raving and roaring as usual , and said to her brother : ` Once I am married to the king 's son I shall be better off than now .
17 I shall take care to have all that pack of courtiers put to death , and then I shall send for all my relations to come and live here instead .
18 I fancy the giants will enjoy themselves very much with me and my husband . '
19 When Prince Ring heard this he fell into such a rage that he ordered the house to be set on fire , and it was burnt to the ground , with the witch and her brother in it .
20 Asmund then told the prince about the two oak trees and took him to see them .
21 The XXXXX was quite astonished at them and at all their contents , but still more so at the extreme beauty of Signy .	prince		brother|ground|mantle|prince|robe|sight|son|tree|trees|trouble

10 The prince was quite astonished at them and at all their contents , but still more so at the extreme beauty of Signy .

12 Asmund , on his side , asked for the hand of Prince Ring 's sister , which was gladly granted him , and the double wedding was celebrated with great rejoicings .
13 After this Prince Asmund and his bride returned to his country to live with the king his father .
14 The two couples often met , and lived happily for many , many years .
15 And that is the end of the story .

17 Rubezahl Over all the vast under-world the mountain Gnome Rubezahl was lord ; and busy enough the care of his dominions kept him .
18 There were the endless treasure chambers to be gone through , and the hosts of gnomes to be kept to their tasks .
19 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
20 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .
21 And as gnomes live many hundreds of years he saw strange XXXXX .	things		brother|ground|house|regions|story|sunshine|things|time|tree|work

13 Rubezahl Over all the vast under-world the mountain Gnome Rubezahl was lord ; and busy enough the care of his dominions kept him .
14 There were the endless treasure chambers to be gone through , and the hosts of gnomes to be kept to their tasks .
15 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
16 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .
17 And as gnomes live many hundreds of years he saw strange things .
18 For , the first time he came up , the great hills were covered with thick forests , in which wild animals roamed , and Rubezahl watched the fierce fights between bear and bison , or chased the grey wolves , or amused himself by rolling great rocks down into the desolate valleys , to hear the thunder of their fall echoing among the hills .
19 But the next time he ventured above ground , what was his surprise to find everything changed !
20 The dark woods were hewn down , and in their place appeared blossoming orchards surrounding cosy-looking thatched cottages ; for every chimney the blue smoke curled peacefully into the air , sheep and oxen fed in the flowery meadows , while from the shade of the hedges came the music of the shepherd 's pipe .

10 Rubezahl Over all the vast under-world the mountain Gnome Rubezahl was lord ; and busy enough the care of his dominions kept him .
11 There were the endless treasure chambers to be gone through , and the hosts of gnomes to be kept to their tasks .
12 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
13 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .

15 For , the first time he came up , the great hills were covered with thick forests , in which wild animals roamed , and Rubezahl watched the fierce fights between bear and bison , or chased the grey wolves , or amused himself by rolling great rocks down into the desolate valleys , to hear the thunder of their fall echoing among the hills .
16 But the next time he ventured above ground , what was his surprise to find everything changed !
17 The dark woods were hewn down , and in their place appeared blossoming orchards surrounding cosy-looking thatched cottages ; for every chimney the blue smoke curled peacefully into the air , sheep and oxen fed in the flowery meadows , while from the shade of the hedges came the music of the shepherd 's pipe .
18 The strangeness and pleasantness of the sight so delighted the gnome that he never thought of resenting the intrusion of these unexpected guests , who , without saying ` by your leave ' or ` with your leave , ' had made themselves so very much at home upon is hills ; nor did he wish to interfere with their doings , but left them in quiet possession of their homes , as a good householder leaves in peace the swallows who have built their nests under his eaves .
19 He was indeed greatly minded to make friends with this being called ` man , ' so , taking the form of an old field labourer , he entered the service of a farmer .
20 Under his care all the crops flourished exceedingly , but the master proved to be wasteful and ungrateful , and Rubezahl soon left him , and went to be shepherd to his next neighbour .
21 He tended the flock so diligently , and knew so well where to lead the sheep to the sweetest pastures , and where among the XXXXX to look for any who strayed away , that they too prospered under his care , and not one was lost or torn by wolves ; but this new master was a hard man , and begrudged him his well-earned wages .	hills		barriers|care|end|farmer|field|gnome|hills|service|stones|while

21 He tended the flock so diligently , and knew so well where to lead the sheep to the sweetest pastures , and where among the hills to look for any who strayed away , that they too prospered under his care , and not one was lost or torn by wolves ; but this new XXXXX was a hard man , and begrudged him his well-earned wages .	master		brother|couples|end|gnome|king|master|possession|regions|service|strangeness

1 When Prince Ring heard this he fell into such a rage that he ordered the house to be set on fire , and it was burnt to the ground , with the witch and her brother in it .
2 Asmund then told the prince about the two oak trees and took him to see them .
3 The prince was quite astonished at them and at all their contents , but still more so at the extreme beauty of Signy .
4 He fell in love with her at once , and entreated her to marry him , which , after a time , she consented to do .
5 Asmund , on his side , asked for the hand of Prince Ring 's sister , which was gladly granted him , and the double wedding was celebrated with great rejoicings .
6 After this Prince Asmund and his bride returned to his country to live with the king his father .
7 The two couples often met , and lived happily for many , many years .
8 And that is the end of the story .
9 -LSB- From Islandische Mahrchen . -RSB-
10 Rubezahl Over all the vast under-world the mountain Gnome Rubezahl was lord ; and busy enough the care of his dominions kept him .
11 There were the endless treasure chambers to be gone through , and the hosts of gnomes to be kept to their tasks .
12 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
13 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .
14 And as gnomes live many hundreds of years he saw strange things .
15 For , the first time he came up , the great hills were covered with thick forests , in which wild animals roamed , and Rubezahl watched the fierce fights between bear and bison , or chased the grey wolves , or amused himself by rolling great rocks down into the desolate valleys , to hear the thunder of their fall echoing among the hills .
16 But the next time he ventured above ground , what was his surprise to find everything changed !
17 The dark woods were hewn down , and in their place appeared blossoming orchards surrounding cosy-looking thatched cottages ; for every chimney the blue smoke curled peacefully into the air , sheep and oxen fed in the flowery meadows , while from the shade of the hedges came the music of the shepherd 's pipe .
18 The strangeness and pleasantness of the sight so delighted the gnome that he never thought of resenting the intrusion of these unexpected guests , who , without saying ` by your leave ' or ` with your leave , ' had made themselves so very much at home upon is hills ; nor did he wish to interfere with their doings , but left them in quiet possession of their homes , as a good householder leaves in peace the swallows who have built their nests under his eaves .
19 He was indeed greatly minded to make friends with this being called ` man , ' so , taking the form of an old field labourer , he entered the service of a farmer .
20 Under his care all the crops flourished exceedingly , but the master proved to be wasteful and ungrateful , and Rubezahl soon left him , and went to be shepherd to his next neighbour .
21 He tended the flock so diligently , and knew so well where to lead the sheep to the sweetest pastures , and where among the hills to look for any who strayed away , that they too prospered under his care , and not one was lost or torn by wolves ; but this new master was a hard XXXXX , and begrudged him his well-earned wages .	man		father|ground|guests|house|king|man|possession|rage|time|witch

3 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
4 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .

6 For , the first time he came up , the great hills were covered with thick forests , in which wild animals roamed , and Rubezahl watched the fierce fights between bear and bison , or chased the grey wolves , or amused himself by rolling great rocks down into the desolate valleys , to hear the thunder of their fall echoing among the hills .
7 But the next time he ventured above ground , what was his surprise to find everything changed !
8 The dark woods were hewn down , and in their place appeared blossoming orchards surrounding cosy-looking thatched cottages ; for every chimney the blue smoke curled peacefully into the air , sheep and oxen fed in the flowery meadows , while from the shade of the hedges came the music of the shepherd 's pipe .
9 The strangeness and pleasantness of the sight so delighted the gnome that he never thought of resenting the intrusion of these unexpected guests , who , without saying ` by your leave ' or ` with your leave , ' had made themselves so very much at home upon is hills ; nor did he wish to interfere with their doings , but left them in quiet possession of their homes , as a good householder leaves in peace the swallows who have built their nests under his eaves .
10 He was indeed greatly minded to make friends with this being called ` man , ' so , taking the form of an old field labourer , he entered the service of a farmer .

13 So he ran away and went to serve the judge .
14 Here he upheld the law with might and main , and was a terror to thieves and evildoers ; but the judge was a bad man , who took bribes , and despised the law .
15 Rubezahl would not be the tool of an unjust man , and so he told his master , who thereupon ordered him to be thrown in prison .
16 Of course that did not trouble the gnome at all , he simply got out through the keyhole , and went away down to his underground palace , very much disappointed by his first experience of mankind .
17 But , as time went on , he forgot the disagreeable things that had happened to him , and thought he would take another look at the upper world .
18 So he stole into the valley , keeping himself carefully hidden in copse or hedgerow , and very soon met with an adventure ; for , peeping through a screen of leaves , he saw before him a green lawn where stood a charming maiden , fresh as the spring , and beautiful to look upon .
19 Around her upon the grass lay her young companions , as if they had thrown themselves down to rest after some merry game .
20 Beyond them flowed a little brook , into which a waterfall leapt from a high rock , filling the air with its pleasant sound , and making a coolness even in the sultry noontide .
21 The XXXXX of the maiden so pleased the gnome that , for the first time , he wished himself a mortal ; and , longing for a better view of the gay company , he changed himself into a raven and perched upon an oaktree which overhung the brook .	sight		animals|fancy|forests|form|gnomes|ground|leave|rest|sight|stones

14 Here he upheld the law with might and main , and was a terror to thieves and evildoers ; but the judge was a bad man , who took bribes , and despised the law .
15 Rubezahl would not be the tool of an unjust man , and so he told his master , who thereupon ordered him to be thrown in prison .
16 Of course that did not trouble the gnome at all , he simply got out through the keyhole , and went away down to his underground palace , very much disappointed by his first experience of mankind .
17 But , as time went on , he forgot the disagreeable things that had happened to him , and thought he would take another look at the upper world .
18 So he stole into the valley , keeping himself carefully hidden in copse or hedgerow , and very soon met with an adventure ; for , peeping through a screen of leaves , he saw before him a green lawn where stood a charming maiden , fresh as the spring , and beautiful to look upon .
19 Around her upon the grass lay her young companions , as if they had thrown themselves down to rest after some merry game .
20 Beyond them flowed a little brook , into which a waterfall leapt from a high rock , filling the air with its pleasant sound , and making a coolness even in the sultry noontide .
21 The sight of the maiden so pleased the gnome that , for the first XXXXX , he wished himself a mortal ; and , longing for a better view of the gay company , he changed himself into a raven and perched upon an oaktree which overhung the brook .	time		earth|forests|grass|guests|home|keyhole|man|sunshine|time|world

1 Rubezahl Over all the vast under-world the mountain Gnome Rubezahl was lord ; and busy enough the care of his dominions kept him .
2 There were the endless treasure chambers to be gone through , and the hosts of gnomes to be kept to their tasks .
3 Some built strong barriers to hold back the fiery vapours to change dull stones to precious metal , or were hard at work filling every cranny of the rocks with diamonds and rubies ; for Rubezahl loved all pretty things .
4 Sometimes the fancy would take him to leave those gloomy regions , and come out upon the green earth for a while , and bask in the sunshine and hear the birds sing .
5 And as gnomes live many hundreds of years he saw strange things .
6 For , the first time he came up , the great hills were covered with thick forests , in which wild animals roamed , and Rubezahl watched the fierce fights between bear and bison , or chased the grey wolves , or amused himself by rolling great rocks down into the desolate valleys , to hear the thunder of their fall echoing among the hills .
7 But the next time he ventured above ground , what was his surprise to find everything changed !
8 The dark woods were hewn down , and in their place appeared blossoming orchards surrounding cosy-looking thatched cottages ; for every chimney the blue smoke curled peacefully into the air , sheep and oxen fed in the flowery meadows , while from the shade of the hedges came the music of the shepherd 's pipe .
9 The strangeness and pleasantness of the sight so delighted the gnome that he never thought of resenting the intrusion of these unexpected guests , who , without saying ` by your leave ' or ` with your leave , ' had made themselves so very much at home upon is hills ; nor did he wish to interfere with their doings , but left them in quiet possession of their homes , as a good householder leaves in peace the swallows who have built their nests under his eaves .
10 He was indeed greatly minded to make friends with this being called ` man , ' so , taking the form of an old field labourer , he entered the service of a farmer .
11 Under his care all the crops flourished exceedingly , but the master proved to be wasteful and ungrateful , and Rubezahl soon left him , and went to be shepherd to his next neighbour .
12 He tended the flock so diligently , and knew so well where to lead the sheep to the sweetest pastures , and where among the hills to look for any who strayed away , that they too prospered under his care , and not one was lost or torn by wolves ; but this new master was a hard man , and begrudged him his well-earned wages .
13 So he ran away and went to serve the judge .
14 Here he upheld the law with might and main , and was a terror to thieves and evildoers ; but the judge was a bad man , who took bribes , and despised the law .
15 Rubezahl would not be the tool of an unjust man , and so he told his master , who thereupon ordered him to be thrown in prison .
16 Of course that did not trouble the gnome at all , he simply got out through the keyhole , and went away down to his underground palace , very much disappointed by his first experience of mankind .
17 But , as time went on , he forgot the disagreeable things that had happened to him , and thought he would take another look at the upper world .
18 So he stole into the valley , keeping himself carefully hidden in copse or hedgerow , and very soon met with an adventure ; for , peeping through a screen of leaves , he saw before him a green lawn where stood a charming maiden , fresh as the spring , and beautiful to look upon .
19 Around her upon the grass lay her young companions , as if they had thrown themselves down to rest after some merry game .
20 Beyond them flowed a little brook , into which a waterfall leapt from a high rock , filling the air with its pleasant sound , and making a coolness even in the sultry noontide .
21 The sight of the maiden so pleased the gnome that , for the first time , he wished himself a mortal ; and , longing for a better view of the gay company , he changed himself into a raven and perched upon an oaktree which overhung the XXXXX .	brook		barriers|birds|brook|companions|earth|homes|master|palace|time|wages

19 Daisies and forget-me-nots fringed its brink , while tall hedges of roses and jasmine ringed it round , making the sweetest and daintiest bower imaginable .
20 To the right and left of the waterfall opened out a wonderful grotto , its walls and arches glittering with many-coloured rock-crystals , while in every niche were spread out strange fruits and sweetmeats , the very sight of which made the princess long to taste them .
21 She hesitated a while , however , scarcely able to believe her XXXXX , and not knowing if she should enter the enchanted spot or fly from it .	eyes		Daisies|company|eyes|forest|look|marble|plan|sight|thicket|way

1 Here he upheld the law with might and main , and was a terror to thieves and evildoers ; but the judge was a bad man , who took bribes , and despised the law .
2 Rubezahl would not be the tool of an unjust man , and so he told his master , who thereupon ordered him to be thrown in prison .
3 Of course that did not trouble the gnome at all , he simply got out through the keyhole , and went away down to his underground palace , very much disappointed by his first experience of mankind .
4 But , as time went on , he forgot the disagreeable things that had happened to him , and thought he would take another look at the upper world .
5 So he stole into the valley , keeping himself carefully hidden in copse or hedgerow , and very soon met with an adventure ; for , peeping through a screen of leaves , he saw before him a green lawn where stood a charming maiden , fresh as the spring , and beautiful to look upon .
6 Around her upon the grass lay her young companions , as if they had thrown themselves down to rest after some merry game .
7 Beyond them flowed a little brook , into which a waterfall leapt from a high rock , filling the air with its pleasant sound , and making a coolness even in the sultry noontide .
8 The sight of the maiden so pleased the gnome that , for the first time , he wished himself a mortal ; and , longing for a better view of the gay company , he changed himself into a raven and perched upon an oaktree which overhung the brook .
9 But he soon found that this was not at all a good plan .
10 He could only see with a raven 's eyes , and feel as a raven feels ; and a nest of field-mice at the foot of the tree interested him far more than the sport of the maidens .
11 When he understood this he flew down again in a great hurry into the thicket , and took the form of a handsome young man -- that was the best way -- and he fell in love with the girl then and there .
12 The fair maiden was the daughter of the king of the country , and she often wandered in the forest with her play fellows gathering the wild flowers and fruits , till the midday heat drove the merry band to the shady lawn by the brook to rest , or to bathe in the cool waters .
13 On this particular morning the fancy took them to wander off again into the wood .
14 This was Master Rubezahl 's opportunity .
15 Stepping out of his hiding-place he stood in the midst of the little lawn , weaving his magic spells , till slowly all about him changed , and when the maidens returned at noon to their favourite resting-place they stood lost in amazement , and almost fancied that they must be dreaming .
16 The red rocks had become white marble and alabaster ; the stream that murmured and struggled before in its rocky bed , flowed in silence now in its smooth channel , from which a clear fountain leapt , to fall again in showers of diamond drops , now on this side now on that , as the wandering breeze scattered it .
17 Daisies and forget-me-nots fringed its brink , while tall hedges of roses and jasmine ringed it round , making the sweetest and daintiest bower imaginable .
18 To the right and left of the waterfall opened out a wonderful grotto , its walls and arches glittering with many-coloured rock-crystals , while in every niche were spread out strange fruits and sweetmeats , the very sight of which made the princess long to taste them .
19 She hesitated a while , however , scarcely able to believe her eyes , and not knowing if she should enter the enchanted spot or fly from it .
20 But at length curiosity prevailed , and she and her companions explored to their heart 's content , and tasted and examined everything , running hither and thither in high glee , and calling merrily to each other .
21 At last , when they were quite weary , the XXXXX cried out suddenly that nothing would content her but to bathe in the marble pool , which certainly did look very inviting ; and they all went gaily to this new amusement .	princess		bed|brink|fruits|girl|grass|keyhole|princess|spot|tree|view

1 So he stole into the valley , keeping himself carefully hidden in copse or hedgerow , and very soon met with an adventure ; for , peeping through a screen of leaves , he saw before him a green lawn where stood a charming maiden , fresh as the spring , and beautiful to look upon .
2 Around her upon the grass lay her young companions , as if they had thrown themselves down to rest after some merry game .
3 Beyond them flowed a little brook , into which a waterfall leapt from a high rock , filling the air with its pleasant sound , and making a coolness even in the sultry noontide .
4 The sight of the maiden so pleased the gnome that , for the first time , he wished himself a mortal ; and , longing for a better view of the gay company , he changed himself into a raven and perched upon an oaktree which overhung the brook .
5 But he soon found that this was not at all a good plan .
6 He could only see with a raven 's eyes , and feel as a raven feels ; and a nest of field-mice at the foot of the tree interested him far more than the sport of the maidens .
7 When he understood this he flew down again in a great hurry into the thicket , and took the form of a handsome young man -- that was the best way -- and he fell in love with the girl then and there .
8 The fair maiden was the daughter of the king of the country , and she often wandered in the forest with her play fellows gathering the wild flowers and fruits , till the midday heat drove the merry band to the shady lawn by the brook to rest , or to bathe in the cool waters .
9 On this particular morning the fancy took them to wander off again into the wood .
10 This was Master Rubezahl 's opportunity .
11 Stepping out of his hiding-place he stood in the midst of the little lawn , weaving his magic spells , till slowly all about him changed , and when the maidens returned at noon to their favourite resting-place they stood lost in amazement , and almost fancied that they must be dreaming .
12 The red rocks had become white marble and alabaster ; the stream that murmured and struggled before in its rocky bed , flowed in silence now in its smooth channel , from which a clear fountain leapt , to fall again in showers of diamond drops , now on this side now on that , as the wandering breeze scattered it .
13 Daisies and forget-me-nots fringed its brink , while tall hedges of roses and jasmine ringed it round , making the sweetest and daintiest bower imaginable .
14 To the right and left of the waterfall opened out a wonderful grotto , its walls and arches glittering with many-coloured rock-crystals , while in every niche were spread out strange fruits and sweetmeats , the very sight of which made the princess long to taste them .
15 She hesitated a while , however , scarcely able to believe her eyes , and not knowing if she should enter the enchanted spot or fly from it .
16 But at length curiosity prevailed , and she and her companions explored to their heart 's content , and tasted and examined everything , running hither and thither in high glee , and calling merrily to each other .
17 At last , when they were quite weary , the princess cried out suddenly that nothing would content her but to bathe in the marble pool , which certainly did look very inviting ; and they all went gaily to this new amusement .
18 The princess was ready first , but scarcely had she slipped over the rim of the pool when down -- down -- down she sank , and vanished in its depths before her frightened playmates could seize her by so much as a lock of her floating golden hair !
19 Loudly did they weep and wail , running about the brink of the pool , which looked so shallow and so clear , but which had swallowed up their princess before their eyes .
20 They even sprang into the water and tried to dive after her , but in vain ; they only floated like corks in the enchanted pool , and could not keep under water for a second .
21 They saw at last that there was nothing for it but to carry to the XXXXX the sad tidings of his beloved daughter 's disappearance .	king		Daisies|amazement|country|hurry|king|marble|midst|playmates|view|water

9 But at length curiosity prevailed , and she and her companions explored to their heart 's content , and tasted and examined everything , running hither and thither in high glee , and calling merrily to each other .
10 At last , when they were quite weary , the princess cried out suddenly that nothing would content her but to bathe in the marble pool , which certainly did look very inviting ; and they all went gaily to this new amusement .
11 The princess was ready first , but scarcely had she slipped over the rim of the pool when down -- down -- down she sank , and vanished in its depths before her frightened playmates could seize her by so much as a lock of her floating golden hair !
12 Loudly did they weep and wail , running about the brink of the pool , which looked so shallow and so clear , but which had swallowed up their princess before their eyes .
13 They even sprang into the water and tried to dive after her , but in vain ; they only floated like corks in the enchanted pool , and could not keep under water for a second .
14 They saw at last that there was nothing for it but to carry to the king the sad tidings of his beloved daughter 's disappearance .
15 And what great weeping and lamentation there was in the palace when the dreadful news was told !
16 The king tore his robes , dashed his golden crown from his head , and hid his face in his purple mantle for grief and anguish at the loss of the princess .
17 After the first outburst of wailing , however , he took heart and hurried off to see for himself the scene of this strange adventure , thinking , as people will in sorrow , that there might be some mistake after all .
18 But when he reached the spot , behold , all was changed again !
19 The glittering grotto described to him by the maidens had completely vanished , and so had the marble bath , the bower of jasmine ; instead , all was a tangle of flowers , as it had been of old .
20 The king was so much perplexed that he threatened the princess 's playfellows with all sorts of punishments if they would not confess something about her disappearance ; but as they only repeated the same story he presently put down the whole affair to the work of some sprite or goblin , and tried to console himself for his loss by ordering a grand hunt ; for kings can not bear to be troubled about anything long .
21 Meanwhile the princess was not at all unhappy in the XXXXX of her elfish lover .	palace		adventure|daughter|forest|king|lamentation|opportunity|palace|pool|sight|waterfall

4 The red rocks had become white marble and alabaster ; the stream that murmured and struggled before in its rocky bed , flowed in silence now in its smooth channel , from which a clear fountain leapt , to fall again in showers of diamond drops , now on this side now on that , as the wandering breeze scattered it .
5 Daisies and forget-me-nots fringed its brink , while tall hedges of roses and jasmine ringed it round , making the sweetest and daintiest bower imaginable .
6 To the right and left of the waterfall opened out a wonderful grotto , its walls and arches glittering with many-coloured rock-crystals , while in every niche were spread out strange fruits and sweetmeats , the very sight of which made the princess long to taste them .

19 The fruit trees were hung with golden and rosy apples , and nightingales sang in every bush , as the gnome and the princess wandered in the leafy alleys , sometimes gazing at the moon , sometimes pausing to gather the rarest flowers for her adornment .
20 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .

16 The gnome , for he it was , told her all about himself and his great underground kingdom , and presently led her through the many rooms and halls of the palace , and showed her the rare and wonderful things displayed in them till she was fairly dazzled at the sight of so much splendour .
17 On three sides of the castle lay a lovely garden with masses of gay , sweet flowers , and velvet lawns all cool and shady , which pleased the eye of the princess .
18 The fruit trees were hung with golden and rosy apples , and nightingales sang in every bush , as the gnome and the princess wandered in the leafy alleys , sometimes gazing at the moon , sometimes pausing to gather the rarest flowers for her adornment .
19 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .
20 But the princess felt no such happiness ; in spite of all the magic delights around her she was sad , though she tried to seem content for fear of displeasing the gnome .
21 However , he soon perceived her melancholy , and in a thousand XXXXX strove to dispel the cloud , but in vain .	ways		dive|kingdom|lamentation|lover|news|pool|spite|spot|time|ways

1 They even sprang into the water and tried to dive after her , but in vain ; they only floated like corks in the enchanted pool , and could not keep under water for a second .
2 They saw at last that there was nothing for it but to carry to the king the sad tidings of his beloved daughter 's disappearance .
3 And what great weeping and lamentation there was in the palace when the dreadful news was told !
4 The king tore his robes , dashed his golden crown from his head , and hid his face in his purple mantle for grief and anguish at the loss of the princess .
5 After the first outburst of wailing , however , he took heart and hurried off to see for himself the scene of this strange adventure , thinking , as people will in sorrow , that there might be some mistake after all .
6 But when he reached the spot , behold , all was changed again !
7 The glittering grotto described to him by the maidens had completely vanished , and so had the marble bath , the bower of jasmine ; instead , all was a tangle of flowers , as it had been of old .
8 The king was so much perplexed that he threatened the princess 's playfellows with all sorts of punishments if they would not confess something about her disappearance ; but as they only repeated the same story he presently put down the whole affair to the work of some sprite or goblin , and tried to console himself for his loss by ordering a grand hunt ; for kings can not bear to be troubled about anything long .
9 Meanwhile the princess was not at all unhappy in the palace of her elfish lover .
10 When the water-nymphs , who were hiding in readiness , had caught her and dragged her out of the sight of her terrified maidens , she herself had not had time to be frightened .
11 They swam with her quickly by strange underground ways to a palace so splendid that her father 's seemed but a poor cottage in comparison with it , and when she recovered from her astonishment she found herself seated upon a couch , wrapped in a wonderful robe of satin fastened with a silken girdle , while beside her knelt a young man who whispered the sweetest speeches imaginable in her ear .
12 The gnome , for he it was , told her all about himself and his great underground kingdom , and presently led her through the many rooms and halls of the palace , and showed her the rare and wonderful things displayed in them till she was fairly dazzled at the sight of so much splendour .
13 On three sides of the castle lay a lovely garden with masses of gay , sweet flowers , and velvet lawns all cool and shady , which pleased the eye of the princess .
14 The fruit trees were hung with golden and rosy apples , and nightingales sang in every bush , as the gnome and the princess wandered in the leafy alleys , sometimes gazing at the moon , sometimes pausing to gather the rarest flowers for her adornment .
15 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .
16 But the princess felt no such happiness ; in spite of all the magic delights around her she was sad , though she tried to seem content for fear of displeasing the gnome .
17 However , he soon perceived her melancholy , and in a thousand ways strove to dispel the cloud , but in vain .
18 At last he said to himself : ` Men are sociable creatures , like bees or ants .
19 Doubtless this lovely mortal is pining for company .

1 Meanwhile the princess was not at all unhappy in the palace of her elfish lover .

5 On three sides of the castle lay a lovely garden with masses of gay , sweet flowers , and velvet lawns all cool and shady , which pleased the eye of the princess .
6 The fruit trees were hung with golden and rosy apples , and nightingales sang in every bush , as the gnome and the princess wandered in the leafy alleys , sometimes gazing at the moon , sometimes pausing to gather the rarest flowers for her adornment .
7 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .
8 But the princess felt no such happiness ; in spite of all the magic delights around her she was sad , though she tried to seem content for fear of displeasing the gnome .
9 However , he soon perceived her melancholy , and in a thousand ways strove to dispel the cloud , but in vain .
10 At last he said to himself : ` Men are sociable creatures , like bees or ants .
11 Doubtless this lovely mortal is pining for company .
12 Who is there I can find for her to talk to ? '

14 ` Loveliest daughter of earth , ' said the gnome , ` banish all sorrow ; no more shall you be lonely in my dwelling .

16 Take this little many-coloured wand , and with a touch give to each root the form you desire to see . '

18 come to me quickly ! '

1 Meanwhile the princess was not at all unhappy in the palace of her elfish lover .
2 When the water-nymphs , who were hiding in readiness , had caught her and dragged her out of the sight of her terrified maidens , she herself had not had time to be frightened .
3 They swam with her quickly by strange underground ways to a palace so splendid that her father 's seemed but a poor cottage in comparison with it , and when she recovered from her astonishment she found herself seated upon a couch , wrapped in a wonderful robe of satin fastened with a silken girdle , while beside her knelt a young man who whispered the sweetest speeches imaginable in her ear .
4 The gnome , for he it was , told her all about himself and his great underground kingdom , and presently led her through the many rooms and halls of the palace , and showed her the rare and wonderful things displayed in them till she was fairly dazzled at the sight of so much splendour .
5 On three sides of the castle lay a lovely garden with masses of gay , sweet flowers , and velvet lawns all cool and shady , which pleased the eye of the princess .
6 The fruit trees were hung with golden and rosy apples , and nightingales sang in every bush , as the gnome and the princess wandered in the leafy alleys , sometimes gazing at the moon , sometimes pausing to gather the rarest flowers for her adornment .
7 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .
8 But the princess felt no such happiness ; in spite of all the magic delights around her she was sad , though she tried to seem content for fear of displeasing the gnome .
9 However , he soon perceived her melancholy , and in a thousand ways strove to dispel the cloud , but in vain .
10 At last he said to himself : ` Men are sociable creatures , like bees or ants .
11 Doubtless this lovely mortal is pining for company .
12 Who is there I can find for her to talk to ? '
13 Thereupon he hastened into the nearest filed and dug up a dozen or so of different roots -- carrots , turnips , and radishes -- and laying them carefully in an elegant basket brought them to the princess , who sat pensive in the shade of the rose-bower .
14 ` Loveliest daughter of earth , ' said the gnome , ` banish all sorrow ; no more shall you be lonely in my dwelling .
15 In this basket is all you need to make this spot delightful to you .
16 Take this little many-coloured wand , and with a touch give to each root the form you desire to see . '
17 With this he left her , and the princess , without an instant 's delay , opened the basket , and touching a turnip , cried eagerly : ` Brunhilda , my dear Brunhilda !
18 come to me quickly ! '
19 And sure enough there was Brunhilda , joyfully hugging and kissing her beloved princess , and chattering as gaily as in the old days .
20 This sudden appearance was so delightful that the princess could hardly believe her own eyes , and was quite beside herself with the joy of having her dear playfellow with her once more .
21 Hand in hand they wandered about the enchanted garden , and gathered the golden apples from the trees , and when they were tired of this amusement the princess led her friend through all the wonderful rooms of the XXXXX , until at last they came to the one in which were kept all the marvellous dresses and ornaments the gnome had given to his hoped-for bride .	palace		basket|bees|gnome|palace|princess|readiness|rooms|things|time|turnips

4 And all the time he was thinking to himself that never , during the hundreds of years he had lived , had he seen so charming a maiden .
5 But the princess felt no such happiness ; in spite of all the magic delights around her she was sad , though she tried to seem content for fear of displeasing the gnome .
6 However , he soon perceived her melancholy , and in a thousand ways strove to dispel the cloud , but in vain .
7 At last he said to himself : ` Men are sociable creatures , like bees or ants .
8 Doubtless this lovely mortal is pining for company .
9 Who is there I can find for her to talk to ? '
10 Thereupon he hastened into the nearest filed and dug up a dozen or so of different roots -- carrots , turnips , and radishes -- and laying them carefully in an elegant basket brought them to the princess , who sat pensive in the shade of the rose-bower .
11 ` Loveliest daughter of earth , ' said the gnome , ` banish all sorrow ; no more shall you be lonely in my dwelling .
12 In this basket is all you need to make this spot delightful to you .
13 Take this little many-coloured wand , and with a touch give to each root the form you desire to see . '
14 With this he left her , and the princess , without an instant 's delay , opened the basket , and touching a turnip , cried eagerly : ` Brunhilda , my dear Brunhilda !
15 come to me quickly ! '

17 This sudden appearance was so delightful that the princess could hardly believe her own eyes , and was quite beside herself with the joy of having her dear playfellow with her once more .
18 Hand in hand they wandered about the enchanted garden , and gathered the golden apples from the trees , and when they were tired of this amusement the princess led her friend through all the wonderful rooms of the palace , until at last they came to the one in which were kept all the marvellous dresses and ornaments the gnome had given to his hoped-for bride .
19 There they found so much to amuse them that the hours passed like minutes .
20 Veils , girdles , and necklaces were tried on and admired , the imitation Brunhilda knew so well how to behave herself , and showed so much taste that nobody would ever have suspected that she was nothing but a turnip after all .
21 The XXXXX , who had secretly been keeping an eye upon them , was very pleased with himself for having so well understood the heart of a woman ; and the princess seemed to him even more charming than before .	gnome		castle|friend|gnome|happiness|joy|kingdom|ornaments|root|touch|wand
